<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2019 577:7788"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-12-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Water lilies belong to the angiosperm order Nymphaeales. Amborellales, Nymphaeales and Austrobaileyales together form the so-called ANA-grade of angiosperms, which are extant representatives of lineages that diverged the&#160;earliest from the lineage leading to the extant mesangiosperms1&#8211;3. Here we report the 409-megabase genome sequence of the blue-petal water lily (Nymphaea colorata). Our phylogenomic analyses support Amborellales and Nymphaeales as successive sister lineages to all other extant angiosperms. The N. colorata genome and 19 other water lily transcriptomes reveal a Nymphaealean whole-genome duplication event, which is shared by Nymphaeaceae and possibly Cabombaceae. Among the genes retained from this whole-genome duplication are homologues of genes that regulate flowering transition and flower development. The broad expression of homologues of floral ABCE genes in N. colorata might support a similarly broadly active ancestral ABCE model of floral organ determination in early angiosperms. Water lilies have evolved attractive floral scents and colours, which are features shared with mesangiosperms, and we identified their putative biosynthetic genes in N. colorata. The chemical compounds and biosynthetic genes behind floral scents suggest that they have evolved in parallel to those in mesangiosperms. Because of its unique phylogenetic position, the N. colorata genome sheds light on the early evolution of angiosperms. The genome of the tropical blue-petal water lily Nymphaea colorata and the transcriptomes from 19 other Nymphaeales species provide insights into the early evolution of angiosperms."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Water lilies belong to the angiosperm order Nymphaeales. Amborellales, Nymphaeales and Austrobaileyales together form the so-called ANA-grade of angiosperms, which are extant representatives of lineages that diverged theÂ earliest from the lineage leading to the extant mesangiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Byng, J. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1227">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1227_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Qiu, Y. L. et al. The earliest angiosperms: evidence from mitochondrial, plastid and nuclear genomes. Nature 402, 404â407 (1999)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1230">3</a></sup>. Here we report the 409-megabase genome sequence of the blue-petal water lily (<i>Nymphaea colorata</i>). Our phylogenomic analyses support Amborellales and Nymphaeales as successive sister lineages to all other extant angiosperms. The <i>N. colorata</i> genome and 19 other water lily transcriptomes reveal a Nymphaealean whole-genome duplication event, which is shared by Nymphaeaceae and possibly Cabombaceae. Among the genes retained from this whole-genome duplication are homologues of genes that regulate flowering transition and flower development. The broad expression of homologues of floral ABCE genes in <i>N. colorata</i> might support a similarly broadly active ancestral ABCE model of floral organ determination in early angiosperms. Water lilies have evolved attractive floral scents and colours, which are features shared with mesangiosperms, and we identified their putative biosynthetic genes in <i>N. colorata</i>. The chemical compounds and biosynthetic genes behind floral scents suggest that they have evolved in parallel to those in mesangiosperms. Because of its unique phylogenetic position, the <i>N. colorata</i> genome sheds light on the early evolution of angiosperms.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Many water lily species, particularly from <i>Nymphaea</i> (Nymphaeaceae), have large and showy flowersÂ andÂ belong to the angiosperms (also called flowering plants). Their aesthetic beauty has captivated notable artists such as the French impressionist Claude Monet. Water lily flowers have limited differentiation in perianths (outer floral organs), but they possess both male and female organs and have diverse scents and colours, similar to many mesangiosperms (core angiosperms, including eudicots, monocots, and magnoliids) (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">1</a>). In addition, some water lilies have short life cycles and enormous numbers of seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chen, F. et al. Water lilies as emerging models for Darwinâs abominable mystery. Hortic. Res. 4, 17051 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1286">4</a></sup>, which increase their potential as a model plant to represent the ANA-grade of angiosperms and to study early evolutionary events within the angiosperms. In particular, <i>N. colorata</i> Peter has a relatively small genome size (2<i>n</i>Â =Â 28 and approximately 400Â Mb) and blue petals that make it popular in breeding programs (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>We report here the genome sequence of <i>N. colorata</i>, obtained using PacBio RSII single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technology. The genome was assembled into 1,429 contigs (with a contig N50 of 2.1Â Mb) and total length of 409Â Mb with 804 scaffolds, 770 of which were anchored onto 14 pseudo-chromosomes (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig5">1</a> and Extended Data Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Tab1">1</a>). Genome completeness was estimated to be 94.4% (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">2</a>). We annotated 31,580 protein-coding genes and predicted repetitive elements with a collective length of 160.4Â Mb, accounting for 39.2% of the genome (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>The <i>N. colorata</i> genome provides an opportunity to resolve the relationships between Amborellales, Nymphaeales and all other extant angiosperms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1a</a>). Using six eudicots, six monocots, <i>N. colorata</i> and <i>Amborella</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1332">5</a></sup>, and each of three gymnosperm species (<i>Ginkgo biloba</i>, <i>Picea abies</i> and <i>Pinus taeda</i>) as an outgroup in turn, we identified 2,169, 1,535 and 1,515 orthologous low-copy nuclear (LCN) genes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1b</a>). Among the LCN gene trees inferred from nucleotide sequences using <i>G. biloba</i> as an outgroup, 62% (294 out of 475 trees) place <i>Amborella</i> as the sister lineage to all other extant angiosperms with bootstrap support greater than 80% (type II, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1c</a>). Using <i>P. abies</i> or <i>P. taeda</i> as the outgroup, <i>Amborella</i> is placed as the sister lineage to the remaining angiosperms in 57% and 54% of the LCN gene trees, respectively. LCN gene trees inferred using amino acid sequences show similar phylogenetic patterns (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogenomic relationships of angiosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Phylogenomic relationships of angiosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="778" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Three different evolutionary relationships among major clades of angiosperms. <b>b</b>, Number of LCN gene trees with different bootstrap support (BS) values based on nucleotide sequences from six eudicots, six monocots, <i>N. colorata</i>, <i>Amborella</i> and three different gymnosperms. <b>c</b>, Comparison of gene trees supporting the three evolutionary relationships using each gymnosperm in turn as the outgroup. The percentage was calculated by dividing the number of type I, II or III trees (BS &gt; 80%) by the total number of trees. <b>d</b>, Summary phylogeny and timescale of 115 plant species. Blue bars at nodes represent 95% credibility intervals of the estimated dates. <b>e</b>, The flowers of the 20 sampled water lilies in NymphaealesÂ used inÂ <b>d</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To minimize the potential shortcomings of sparse taxon sampling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wiens, J. J. Missing data, incomplete taxa, and phylogenetic accuracy. Syst. Biol. 52, 528â538 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1418">6</a></sup>, we also inferred an angiosperm species tree using sequences from 44 genomes and 71 transcriptomes, including representatives of the ANA-grade, eudicots, magnoliids, monocots and a gymnosperm outgroup (<i>Gnetum montanum</i>, <i>G. biloba</i>, <i>P. abies</i> and <i>P. taeda</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Sec2">Methods</a>). For further phylogenetic inference of these 115 species, we selected, based on various criteria, five different LCN gene sets including 1,167, 834, 683, 602 and 445 genes. Analyses of these five datasets all yielded similar tree topologies with <i>Amborella</i> and Nymphaeales as successive sister lineages to all other extant angiosperms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1d, e</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.2</a>).</p><p>Molecular dating of angiosperm lineages, using a stringent set of 101 LCN genes and with age calibrations based on 21 fossils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Coiro, M., Doyle, J. A. &amp; Hilton, J. How deep is the conflict between molecular and fossil evidence on the age of angiosperms? New Phytol. 223, 83â99 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1451">7</a></sup>, inferred the crown age of angiosperms at 234â263Â million years ago (Ma) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1d</a>). The split between monocots and eudicots was estimated at 171â203Â Ma and that between Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae at 147â185Â Ma.</p><p>Genomic collinearity unveiled evidence of a whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in <i>N. colorata</i> (Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig5">1f</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig6">2a</a> and Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.1</a>). The number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) distributions for <i>N. colorata</i> paralogues further showed a signature peak at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> of approximately 0.9 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2a</a>) and peaks at similar <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values were identified in other Nymphaeaceae species (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.2</a>), which suggests an ancient single WGD event that is probably shared among Nymphaeaceae members. Comparison of the <i>N. colorata</i> paralogue <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions of orthologues (representing speciation events) between <i>N. colorata</i> and other Nymphaeales lineages, <i>Illicium henryi</i>, and <i>Amborella</i> suggests that the WGD occurred just after the divergence between Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2a</a>). By contrast, phylogenomic analyses of gene families that contained at least one paralogue pair from collinear regions of <i>N. colorata</i> suggest that the WGD is shared between Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2b</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.4</a>). If true, <i>Cabomba caroliniana</i> seems to have retained few duplicates (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2b, c</a>), which would explain the absence of a clear peak in the <i>C. caroliniana</i> paralogue <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.2</a>). Absolute dating of the paralogues of <i>N. colorata</i> does suggest that the WGD could have occurred before or close to the divergence between Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig6">2d</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.3</a>), considering the variable substitution rates among Nymphaealean lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2a, b</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig6">2c</a>). An alternative interpretation of the above results could be that the WGD signatures were from an allopolyploidy event that occurred between ancestral Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae lineages shortly after their divergence and that gave rise to the Nymphaeaceae (but not Cabombaceae) stem lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="A Nymphaealean WGD shared by Nymphaeaceae and possibly Cabombaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: A Nymphaealean WGD shared by Nymphaeaceae and possibly Cabombaceae.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="311" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> age distributions for paralogues found in collinear regions (anchor pairs) of <i>N. colorata</i> and for orthologues between <i>N. colorata</i> and selected Nymphaealean and angiosperm species. Red and yellow arrows indicate under- and overestimations of the <i>N. colorata</i>â<i>Nuphar advena</i> and <i>N. colorata</i>â<i>C. caroliniana</i> divergence, respectively. <b>b</b>, WGD phylogenomic analysis. Numbers in parentheses are the number of gene families with retained <i>C. caroliniana</i> duplicates supporting the duplication events. Numbers below branches show branch lengths in <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> units. The double-arrowed line denotes total <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> from the pointed node to <i>N. colorata</i>.Â We used <i>G.</i> <i>biloba</i> (dashed branch) as an outgroup. The red dot denotes the branch on which most of the anchor pairs in <i>N. colorata</i> coalesced. All mapped duplication events have BSÂ â¥Â 80% in the gene trees. <b>c</b>, Left, the scenario of a WGD (yellow four-pointed star) before the divergence between Nymphaeaceae and Cabombaceae. Right, a possible gene tree under this scenario, with loss of one duplicate in <i>C. caroliniana</i>. Two red dots show where the anchor pair of <i>N. colorata</i> would coalesce. <b>d</b>, Left, scenario of a WGD in the stem lineage of Nymphaeaceae involving an allotetraploid (green four-pointed star) that formed between two ancestral parents after the divergence of the lineages leading to <i>N. colorata</i> and <i>C. caroliniana</i>, with one of the parents being more closely related to <i>C. caroliniana</i>. Right, a gene tree under such a scenario. Red dots are as in <b>c</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The water lily lineage descended from one of the early divergences among angiosperms, before the radiation of mesangiosperms. Thus, this group offers a unique window into the early evolution of angiosperms, particularly that of the flower. We identified 70 MADS-box genes, including homologues of the genes forÂ the ABCE model of floral organ identities: <i>AP1</i> (andÂ also <i>FUL</i>) and <i>AGL6</i> (A function for sepals and petals), <i>AP3</i> and <i>PI</i> (B function for petals and stamen), <i>AG</i> (C function for stamen and carpel), and <i>SEP1</i> (E function for interacting with ABC function proteins). Phylogenetic and collinearity analyses of the MADS-box genes and their genomic neighbourhood indicate that an ancient tandem duplication before the divergence of seed plants gave birth to the ancestors of A function (<i>FUL</i>) and E function genes (<i>SEP</i>) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig7">3</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">6.1</a>). Also, owing to the Nymphaealean WGD, <i>N. colorata</i> has two paralogues, <i>AGa</i> and <i>AGb</i> of the C-function gene <i>AG</i> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig8">4</a>). Similarly, the Nymphaealean WGD-derived duplicates are homologous to other genes associated with development of carpel and stamen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Alvarez-Buylla, E. R. et al. Flower development. Arabidopsis Book 8, e0127 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1720">8</a></sup>, and to genes that regulate flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhao, N. et al. Identification of flowering regulatory genes in allopolyploid Brassica juncea. Hortic. Plant J. 5, 109â119 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1725">9</a></sup> and auxin-controlled circadian opening and closureÂ of the flower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ke, M. et al. Auxin controls circadian flower opening and closure in the waterlily. BMC Plant Biol. 18, 143 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1729">10</a></sup> (Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig8">4</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig10">6</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">6.2</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">6.4</a>).</p><p>The expression profiles of <i>N. colorata</i> ABCE homologues largely agree with their putative ascribed roles in floral organ patterning (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig3">3a</a>). Notably, the <i>N. colorata AGL6</i> homologue is mainly expressed in sepals and petals, whereas the <i>FUL</i> homologue is mainly expressed in carpels, suggesting that <i>AGL6</i> acts as an A-function gene in <i>N. colorata</i>. The two C-function homologues <i>AGa</i> and <i>AGb</i> are highly expressed in stamens and carpels, respectively, whereas <i>AGb</i> is also expressed in sepals and petals, suggesting that they might have undergone subfunctionalization and possibly neofunctionalization for flower development after the Nymphaealean WGD. Furthermore, the ABCE homologues in <i>N. colorata</i> generally exhibit wider ranges of expression in floral organs than their counterparts in eudicot model systems (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig3">3b</a>). This wider expression pattern, in combination with broader expression of at least some ABCE genes in some eudicots representing an early-diverging lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sharma, B. &amp; Kramer, E. M. Aquilegia B gene homologs promote petaloidy of the sepals and maintenance of the C domain boundary. Evodevo 8, 22 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1783">11</a></sup>, some monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dodsworth, S. Petal, sepal, or tepal? B-genes and monocot flowers. Trends Plant Sci. 22, 8â10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1787">12</a></sup> and magnoliids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chanderbali, A. S. et al. Conservation and canalization of gene expression during angiosperm diversification accompany the origin and evolution of the flower. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22570â22575 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1791">13</a></sup>, suggest an ancient ABCE model for flower development, with subsequent canalization of gene expression and function regulated by the more specialized ABCE genes during the evolution of mesangiosperms, especially core eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Alvarez-Buylla, E. R. et al. Flower development. Arabidopsis Book 8, e0127 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1795">8</a></sup>. This could also account for the limited differentiation between sepals and petals in Nymphaeales species, and is consistent with a single type of perianth organ proposed in an ancestral angiosperm flower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sauquet, H. et al. The ancestral flower of angiosperms and its early diversification. Nat. Commun. 8, 16047 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1799">14</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="MADS-box genesÂ inÂ N.Â colorata and proposed floral ABCE model in early angiosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: MADS-box genesÂ inÂ <i>N.Â colorata</i> and proposed floral ABCE model in early angiosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="578" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Gene expression patterns of MIKC<sup>c</sup> from various organs of <i>N. colorata</i>. Three clusters of genes were classified according to the expression of type II MADS-box genes. The organ types (vegetative organs and floral organs) were matched to the expression patterns of type II MADS-box genes. Expression values were scaled by log<sub>2</sub>(FPKMÂ +Â 1), in which FPKM is fragments per kilobase of exon per million mappedÂ reads. <b>b</b>, The flowering ABCE model in <i>N. colorata</i> that specifies floral organs is proposed based on the gene expression values (bar heights) from <b>a</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Floral scent serves as olfactory cues for insect pollinators<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kessler, D. et al. How scent and nectar influence floral antagonists and mutualists. eLife 4, e07641 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1849">15</a></sup>. Whereas <i>Amborella</i> flowers are scentless<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Thien, L. B. et al. The population structure and floral biology of Amborella trichopoda (Amborellaceae). Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 90, 466â490 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1856">16</a></sup>, <i>N. colorata</i> flowers release 11 different volatile compounds, including terpenoids (sesquiterpenes), fatty-acid derivatives (methyl decanoate) and benzenoids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig4">4a</a>). The <i>N. colorata</i> genome contains 92 putative terpene synthase (<i>TPS</i>) genes, which are ascribed to four previously recognized TPS subfamilies in angiosperms: TPS-b, TPS-c, TPS-e/f and TPS-g (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig4">4b</a>), but none was found for TPS-a, which is responsible for sesquiterpene biosynthesis in mesangiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212â229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1876">17</a></sup>. Notably, TPS-b contains more than 80 genes in <i>N. colorata</i>; NC11G0123420 is highly expressed in flowers (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig11">7</a>); this result suggestsÂ that it may be a candidate gene for sesquiterpene biosynthase in <i>N. colorata</i>. Also, methyl decanoate has not been detected as a volatile compound in monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Knudsen, J. T., Tollsten, L. &amp; Bergstrom, L. G. Floral scents-a checklist of volatile compounds isolated by head-space techniques. Phytochemistry 33, 253â280 (1993)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1890">18</a></sup> and is thought to be synthesized in <i>N. colorata</i> by the SABATH family of methyltransferases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhao, N. et al. Structural, biochemical, and phylogenetic analyses suggest that indole-3-acetic acid methyltransferase is an evolutionarily ancient member of the SABATH family. Plant Physiol. 146, 455â467 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17391e1897">19</a></sup>. The <i>N. colorata</i> genome contains 13 <i>SABATH</i> homologues and 12 of them form a Nymphaeales-specific group (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">41</a>). Among these 12 members, NC11G0120830 showed the highest expression in petals (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig4">4c</a>) and its corresponding recombinant protein was demonstrated to beÂ a fatty acid methyltransferase that had the highest activity with decanoic acid as the substrate (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig4">4d</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">7.1</a>). These results suggest that the floral scent biosynthesis in <i>N. colorata</i> has been accomplished through enzymatic functions that have evolved independently from those in mesangiosperms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig4">4e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Floral scent and biosynthesis in N. colorata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Floral scent and biosynthesis in <i>N. colorata</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="518" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Gas chromatogram of floral volatiles from the flower of <i>N. colorata</i>. The internal standard (IS) is nonyl acetate. Methyl esters are in blue; terpenes are in red. Floral scent was measured three times independently with similar results. <b>b</b>, Phylogenetic tree of terpene synthases from <i>N. colorata</i> and representative plants showing the subfamilies from aâh and x. <b>c</b>, Expression analysis of <i>SABATH</i> genes of <i>N. colorata</i> showed that NC11G0120830 had the highest expression level in petal.<b>d</b>, Relative activity of <i>Escherichia coli</i>-expressed NC11G0120830 with six fatty acids as substrates, with the activity on decanoic acid set at 1.0. Data are meanÂ Â±Â s.d. of three independent measurements. <b>e</b>, The presence (+) and absence (â) of sesquiterpenes and methyl decanoate as floral scent compounds and their respective biosynthetic genes in four major lineages of angiosperms when known. <i>DAMT</i>, decanoic acid methyltranferase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Nymphaea colorata</i> is valued for the aesthetically attractive blue colour of petals, which is a rare trait in ornamentals. To understand the molecular basis of the blue colour, we identified delphinidin 3â²-<i>O</i>-(2â³-<i>O</i>-galloyl-6â³-<i>O</i>-acetyl-Î²-galactopyranoside) as the main blue anthocyanidin pigment (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig12">8aâc</a>). By comparing the expression profiles between two <i>N. colorata</i> cultivars with white and blue petals for genes in a reconstructed anthocyanidin biosynthesis pathway, we found genes for an anthocyanidin synthase and a delphinidin-modification enzyme, the expression of which was significantly higher in blue petals than in white petals (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig12">8d, e</a>). These two enzymes catalyse the last two steps of anthocyanidin biosynthesisÂ and are therefore key enzymes specialized in blue pigment biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chen, W. H. et al. Downregulation of putative UDP-glucose: flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase gene alters flower coloring in Phalaenopsis. Plant Cell Rep. 30, 1007â1017 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2008">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wu, Q. et al. Transcriptome sequencing and metabolite analysis for revealing the blue flower formation in waterlily. BMC Genomics 17, 897 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2011">21</a></sup> (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">7.2</a>).</p><p>Water lilies have a global distribution that includes cold regions (northern China and northern Canada), unlike the other ANA-grade angiosperms <i>Amborella</i> (Pacific Islands) and Austrobaileyales (temperate and tropical regions). We detected marked expansions of genes related to immunity and stress responses in <i>N. colorata</i>, including genesÂ encodingÂ nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat (NLR) proteins, protein kinases and WRKY transcription factors, compared with those in <i>Amborella</i> and some mesangiosperms (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig13">9</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">8</a>). It is possible that increased numbers of these genes enabled water lilies to adapt to various ecological habitats globally.</p><p>In conclusion, the <i>N. colorata</i> genome offers a reference for comparative genomics and for resolving the deep phylogenetic relationships among the ANA-grade and mesangiosperms. It has also revealed a WGD specific to Nymphaeales, and provides insights into the early evolution of angiosperms on key innovations such as flower development and floral scent and colour.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Total DNA for genome sequencing was extracted from young leaves. Leaf RNA was extracted from 18 water lily species: <i>N. colorata</i>, <i>Euryale ferox</i>, <i>Brasenia schreberi</i>, <i>Victoria cruziana</i>, <i>Nymphaea mexicana</i>, <i>Nymphaea prolifera</i>, <i>Nymphaea tetragona</i>, <i>Nymphaea potamophila</i>, <i>Nymphaea caerulea</i>, <i>NymphaeaÂ rubra</i>, <i>N</i><i>ymphaea</i> âmidnightâ, <i>Nymphaea</i> âChoolarpâ, <i>N</i><i>ymphaea</i> âParameeâ, <i>N</i><i>ymphaea</i> âWoods blue goddessâ, <i>Nymphaea gigantea</i> âAlbert de Lestangâ, <i>N. gigantea</i> âHybrid Iâ, <i>Nymphaea</i> âThong Garnjanaâ and <i>Nuphar lutea</i>. In addition, for transcriptome sequencing we sampled several organs and tissues from <i>N. colorata</i> including mature leaf, mature leafstalk, juvenile flower, juvenile leaf, juvenile leafstalk, carpel, stamen, sepal, petal and root.</p><p>For PacBio sequencing, we prepared approximately 20-kb SMRTbell libraries. A total of 34 SMRT cells and 49.8 Gb data composed of 5.5 million reads were sequenced on PacBio RSII system with P6-C4 chemistry. All transcriptome libraries were sequenced using the Illumina platform, generating paired-end reads. For the Hi-C sequencing and scaffolding, a Hi-C library was created from tender leaves of <i>N. colorata</i>. In brief, the leaves were fixed with formaldehyde and lysed, and the cross-linked DNA was then digested with MboI overnight. Sticky ends were biotinylated and proximity-ligated to form chimeric junctions, which were physically sheared to and enriched for sizes of 500â700 bp. Chimeric fragments representing the original cross-linked long-distance physical interactions were then processed into paired-end sequencing libraries and 346 million 150-bp paired-end reads, which were sequenced on the Illumina platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec4">Sequence assembly and gene annotation</h3><p>To assemble the 49.8 Gb data composed of 5.5 million reads, we filtered the reads to remove organellar DNA, reads of poor quality or short length, and chimaeras. The contig-level assembly was performed on full PacBio long reads using the Canu package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2135">22</a></sup>. Canu v.1.3 was used for self-correction and assembly. We then polished the draft assembly using Arrow (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>). To increase the accuracy of the assembly, Illumina short reads were recruited for further polishing with the Pilon program (<a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon</a>). The genome assembly quality was measured using BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologues)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2153">23</a></sup> v.3.0. The paired-end reads from Hi-C were uniquely mapped onto the draft assembly contigs, which were grouped into chromosomes and scaffolded using the software Lachesis (<a href="https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS">https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS</a>).</p><p>Genscan (<a href="http://genes.mit.edu/GENSCAN.html">http://genes.mit.edu/GENSCAN.html</a>) and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465âW467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2174">24</a></sup> were used to carry out de novo predictions with gene model parameters trained from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Furthermore, gene models were de novo predicted using MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2181">25</a></sup>. We then evaluated the genes by comparing MAKER results with the corresponding transcript evidence to select gene models that were the most consistent on the basis of an AED metric.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec5">The evolutionary position of water lily and divergence-time estimation</h3><p>LCN genes were identified based on OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2193">26</a></sup> results. The orthologues were obtained from six monocots (<i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, <i>Zostera marina</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>) and six eudicots (<i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> and <i>Beta vulgaris</i>), <i>N. colorata</i>, <i>Amborella</i>, and the gymnosperms <i>G. biloba</i>, <i>P. abies</i> and <i>P. taeda</i>. LCN genes needed to meet the following requirements: strictly single-copy in <i>N. colorata</i>, <i>Amborella</i>, <i>G. biloba</i>, <i>P. abies</i> or <i>P. taeda</i>, and single-copy in at least five of the 12 eudicots or monocots. With <i>G. biloba</i>, <i>P. abies</i> or <i>P. taeda</i> as the outgroup, we identified 2,169, 1,535 and 1,515 orthologous LCN genes, respectively. Furthermore, we trimmed the sites with less than 90% coverage. LCN gene trees were estimated from the remaining sites using RAxML v.7.7.8 using the GTR+G+I model for nucleotide sequences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1c</a>) and the JTT+G+I model for amino acid sequences (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.1</a>). To account for incomplete lineage sorting and different substitution rates, we applied the multispecies coalescent model and a supermatrix method, respectively, to the LCN genes and found further support for the sister relationship between <i>Amborella</i> and all other extant flowering plants (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.2</a>).</p><p>We further carefully selected five LCN gene sets (1,167, 834, 683, 602 and 445) from 115 species and applied both a supermatrix method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. 214, 1338â1354 (2017)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2292">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiang, Y. et al. Evolution of Rosaceae fruit types based on nuclear phylogeny in the context of geological times and genome duplication. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 262â281 (2017)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2292_1">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang, C. H. et al. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 394â412 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2295">29</a></sup> and the multi-species coalescent model to infer the phylogeny of angiosperms (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.2</a>). The phylogeny inferred from 1,167 LCN genes is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1d</a>, with different support values from the multi-species coalescent analyses of the other four LCN gene sets.</p><p>To estimate the evolutionary timescale of angiosperms, we calibrated a relaxed molecular clock using 21 fossil-based age constraints<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Coiro, M., Doyle, J. A. &amp; Hilton, J. How deep is the conflict between molecular and fossil evidence on the age of angiosperms? New Phytol. 223, 83â99 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2308">7</a></sup> throughout the tree, including the earliest fossil tricoplate pollen (approximately 125 Ma) associated with eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Hickey, L. J. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Early cretaceous fossil evidence for angisperm evolution. Bot. Rev. 43, 3â104 (1977)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2312">30</a></sup>. We concatenated 101 selected genes (205,185 sites) and fixed the tree topology to that inferred from our coalescent-based analysis of 1,167 genes from 115 taxa. We performed a Bayesian phylogenomic dating analysis of the 101 selected genes in MCMCtree, part of the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2316">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274âE2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2319">32</a></sup>, and used approximate likelihood calculation for the branch lengths<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="dos Reis, M. &amp; Yang, Z. Approximate likelihood calculation on a phylogeny for Bayesian estimation of divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2161â2172 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2323">33</a></sup>. Molecular dating was performed using an auto-correlated model of among-lineage rate variation, the GTR substitution model, and a uniform prior on the relative node times. Posterior distributions of node ages were estimated using Markov chain Monte Carlo sampling, with samples drawn every 250 steps over 10 million steps following a burn-in of 500,000 steps. We checked for convergence by running the analysis in duplicate and checked for sufficient sampling.</p><p>We also implemented the penalized likelihood method under a variable substitution rate using TreePL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Smith, S. A. &amp; OâMeara, B. C. treePL: divergence time estimation using penalized likelihood for large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 28, 2689â2690 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2330">34</a></sup> and r8s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Sanderson, M. J. r8s: inferring absolute rates of molecular evolution and divergence times in the absence of a molecular clock. Bioinformatics 19, 301â302 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2334">35</a></sup>, as a constant substitution rate across the phylogenetic tree was rejected (<i>P</i>Â &lt;Â 0.01) for all cases by likelihood-ratio tests in PAUP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wilgenbusch, J. C. &amp; Swofford, D. Inferring evolutionary trees with PAUP*. Curr. Prot. BioinformaticsÂ 6, 6.4.1â6.4.28 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2341">36</a></sup>. Three fossil calibrations, corresponding to the crown groups of Lamiales, Cornales and Laurales, were implemented as minimum age constraints in our penalized likelihood dating analysis, except that the earliest appearance of tricolpate pollen grains (about 125 Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Hickey, L. J. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Early cretaceous fossil evidence for angisperm evolution. Bot. Rev. 43, 3â104 (1977)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2345">30</a></sup> was used to fix the age of crown eudicots. We determined the best smoothing parameter value of the concatenated 101 LCN genes as 0.32 by performing cross-validations of a range of smooth parameters from 0.01 to 10,000 (algorithmÂ =Â TN; crossvÂ =Â yes; cvstartÂ =Â â2; cvincÂ =Â 0.5; cvnumÂ =Â 15). We used 100 bootstrap trees with branch lengths generated by RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688â2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2350">37</a></sup> to infer the 95% confidence intervals of age estimates (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">4.2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec6">Identification of WGD</h3><p>The <i>N. colorata</i> genome was compared with each of the other genomes by pairwise alignment using Large-Scale Genome Alignment Tool (LAST; <a href="http://last.cbrc.jp/">http://last.cbrc.jp/</a>). We defined syntenic blocks using LAST hits with a distance cut-off of 20 genes apart from the two retained homologous pairs, in which at least four consecutive retained homologous pairs were required. We then obtained the one-to-one blocks to exclude ancient duplication blocks with QUOTA-ALIGN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2375">38</a></sup>.</p><p><i>K</i><sub>S</sub>-based paralogue age distributions were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177â190 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2385">39</a></sup>. In brief, the paranome was constructed by performing an all-against-all protein sequence similarity search using BLASTP with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 10<sup>â10</sup>, after which gene families were built with the mclblastline pipeline (v.10-201) (micans.org/mcl). Each gene family was aligned using MUSCLE (v.3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2394">40</a></sup>, and <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for all pairwise comparisons within a gene family were obtained using maximum likelihood in the CODEML program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol. 11, 725â736 (1994)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2403">41</a></sup> of the PAML package (v.4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2407">31</a></sup>. We then subdivided gene families into subfamilies for which <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between members did not exceed a value of 5.</p><p>To correct for the redundancy of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values (a gene family of <i>n</i> members produces <i>n</i>(<i>n</i>Â âÂ 1)/2 pairwise <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for <i>nÂ â</i>Â 1 retained duplication events), we inferred a phylogenetic tree for each subfamily using PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2439">42</a></sup> with the default settings. For each duplication node in the resulting phylogenetic tree, all <i>m K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between the two child clades were added to the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution with a weight of 1/<i>m</i> (in which <i>m</i> is the number of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a duplication event), so that the weights of all <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a single duplication event summed to one. Paralogous gene pairs found in duplicated collinear segments (anchor pairs) from <i>N. colorata</i> were detected using i-ADHoRe (v.3.0) with âlevel_2_onlyÂ =Â TRUEâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0âfast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2470">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Fostier, J. et al. A greedy, graph-based algorithm for the alignment of multiple homologous gene lists. Bioinformatics 27, 749â756 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2473">44</a></sup>. The identified anchor pairs are assumed to correspond to the most recent WGD event.</p><p>The <i>K</i><sub>S</sub>-based orthologue age distributions were constructed by identifying one-to-one orthologues between species using InParanoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196âD203 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2484">45</a></sup> with default settings, followed by <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimation using the CODEML program as above. <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions for one-to-one orthologues between <i>N. colorata</i> and each of <i>V. cruziana</i>, <i>N. advena</i>, <i>C. caroliniana</i>, <i>I. henryi</i> and <i>Amborella</i> were used to compare the relative timing of the WGD in <i>N. colorata</i> with speciation events within Nymphaeales. <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions for one-to-one orthologues between the outgroup species <i>I. henryi</i> and each of <i>N. lutea</i>, <i>N. advena</i>, <i>N. mexicana</i>, <i>Nymphaea</i> âWoods blue goddessâ, <i>N. colorata</i>, and <i>C. caroliniana</i> were used to estimate and compare relative substitution rates among these Nymphaealean species. Additional comparisons using <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>Amborella</i> as outgroup species instead of <i>I. henryi</i> gave similar results (data not shown).</p><p>Absolute dating of the identified WGD event in <i>N. colorata</i> was performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchids. Nature 549, 379â383 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2562">46</a></sup>. Briefly, paralogous gene pairs located in duplicated segments (anchor pairs) and duplicated pairs lying under the WGD peak (peak-based duplicates) were collected for phylogenetic dating. We selected anchor pairs and peak-based duplicates present under the <i>N. colorata</i> WGD peak and with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values between 0.7 and 1.2 (grey-shaded area in Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig6">2b</a>) for absolute dating. For each WGD paralogous pair, an orthogroup was created that included the two paralogues plus several orthologues from other plant species as identified by InParanoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196âD203 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2577">45</a></sup> using a broad taxonomic sampling: one representative orthologue from the order Cucurbitales, two from Rosales, two from Fabales, two from Malpighiales, two from Brassicales, one from Malvales, one from Solanales, two from Poaceae (Poales), one from <i>A. comosus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2583">47</a></sup> (Bromeliaceae, Poales), one from either <i>M. acuminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="DâHont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213â217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2589">48</a></sup> (Zingiberales) or <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2595">49</a></sup> (Arecales), one from the Asparagales (from <i>Asparagus officinalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Harkess, A. et al. Commun. 5, 3311 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2620">52</a></sup> or <i>Z. marina</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Olsen, J. Nature 530, 331â335 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2627">53</a></sup>), one from <i>Amborella</i>, and one from <i>G. biloba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Guan, R. et al. In total, 217 orthogroups based on anchor pairs and 142 orthogroups based on peak-based duplicates were collected.</p><p>The node joining the two WGD paralogues of <i>N. colorata</i> was then dated using the BEAST v1.7 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2646">55</a></sup> under an uncorrelated relaxed-clock model and an LG+G model with four site-rate categories. A starting tree with branch lengths satisfying all fossil prior constraints was created according to the consensus APG IV phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Byng, J. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2650">1</a></sup>. Fossil calibrations were implemented using log-normal calibration priors on the following nodes: the node uniting the Malvidae based on the fossil <i>Dressiantha bicarpellata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Gandolfo, M., Nixon, K. &amp; Crepet, W. Bot. 85, 964 (1998)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2656">56</a></sup> with prior offsetÂ =Â 82.8, meanÂ =Â 3.8528, and s.d. =Â 0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Beilstein, M. A., Nagalingum, N. S., Clements, M. D., Manchester, S. R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724â18728 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2660">57</a></sup>; the node uniting the Fabidae based on the fossil <i>Paleoclusia chevalieri</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Crepet, W. &amp; Nixon, K. Fossil Clusiaceae from the late Cretaceous (Turonian) of New Jersey and implications regarding the history of bee pollination. Am. J. Bot. 85, 1122â1133 (1998)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2667">58</a></sup> with prior offsetÂ =Â 82.8, meanÂ =Â 3.9314, and s.d. =Â 0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Xi, Z. et al.
