R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R. B., Marshall, C. R. &amp; Pierce, N. E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2677">60</a></sup> with prior offsetÂ =Â 93.0, meanÂ =Â 3.5458, and s.d. =Â 0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385â398 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2681">61</a></sup>; the node uniting the <i>N. colorata</i> WGD paralogues with the eudicots and monocots based on the sudden abundant appearance of eudicot tricolpate pollen in the fossil record with prior offsetÂ =Â 124, meanÂ =Â 4.8143 and s.d. =Â 0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Smith, S. A., Beaulieu, J. M. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. An uncorrelated relaxed-clock analysis suggests an earlier origin for flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 5897â5902 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2689">62</a></sup>; and the root uniting the above clades with <i>Amborella</i> and then <i>G. biloba</i> with prior offsetÂ =Â 307, meanÂ =Â 3.8876, and s.d. =Â 0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. M. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. J. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266â301 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2699">63</a></sup>. The offsets of these calibrations represent hard minimum boundaries, and their means represent locations for their respective peak mass probabilities in accordance with previous dating studies of these specific clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. M. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. J. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266â301 (2011)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2703">63</a></sup> (see Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.3</a> for an alternative setting of orthogroups).</p><p>A run without data was performed to ensure proper placements of the marginal calibration priors, which do not necessarily correspond to the calibration priors specified above, because they interact with each other and the tree prior<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Heled, J. &amp; Drummond, A. J. Calibrated tree priors for relaxed phylogenetics and divergence time estimation. Syst. Biol. 61, 138â149 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2713">64</a></sup>. Indeed, a run without data indicated that the distribution of the marginal calibration prior for the root did not correspond to the specified calibration density, so we reduced the mean in the calibration prior of the node combining the <i>N. colorata</i> WGD paralogues with the eudicots and monocots with offsetÂ =Â 124, meanÂ =Â 4.4397, s.d. =Â 0.5 to locate the marginal calibration prior at 220 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Smith, S. A., Beaulieu, J. M. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. An uncorrelated relaxed-clock analysis suggests an earlier origin for flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 5897â5902 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2720">62</a></sup>.</p><p>Markov chain Monte Carlo sampling for each orthogroup was run for 10 million steps, with sampling every 1,000 steps to produce a sample size of 10,000. The resulting trace files were inspected using Tracer v.1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2727">55</a></sup>, with a burn-in of 1,000 samples, to check for convergence and sufficient sampling (minimum effective sample size of 200 for all parameters). In total, 263 orthogroups were accepted, and absolute age estimates of the node uniting the WGD paralogous pairs based on both anchor pairs and peak-based duplicates were grouped into one absolute age distribution, for which kernel density estimation and a bootstrapping procedure were used to find the peak consensus WGD age estimate and its 90% confidence interval boundaries, respectively. More detailed methods have been previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177â190 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2731">39</a></sup>.</p><p>To identify the duplication events that resulted in the 2,648 anchor pairs detected in the genome of <i>N. colorata</i>, we performed phylogenomic analyses to determine the timing of the duplication events relative to the lineage divergences in Nymphaeales as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchids. Nature 549, 379â383 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2741">46</a></sup>. Protein-coding genes from 12 species were used, including eight species from Nymphaeaceae and one species from Cabombaceae in Nymphaeales, one species (<i>I. henryi</i>) from Austrobaileyales, plus <i>Amborella</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>. The phylogeny of the 12 species was obtained from Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1d</a>, and the branch lengths in <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> units were estimated from 23 LCN genes (selected from the 101 LCN genes used in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig1">1d</a>, because only 23 are shared across all of the species studied) using PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2765">31</a></sup> under the free-ratio model. OrthoMCL (v.2.0.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2769">65</a></sup> was used with default parameters to identify gene families. Then, we removed 907 of the 2,648 anchor pairs with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values greater than five. If the remaining anchor pairs fell into different gene families, thus indicating incorrect assignment of gene families by OrthoMCL, we merged the corresponding gene families and finally obtained 53,243 multi-gene gene families. Next, phylogenetic trees were constructed for a subset of 881 gene families with no more than 200 genes that had at least one pair of anchors and one gene from <i>G. biloba</i>. Multiple sequence alignments were produced by MUSCLE (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2781">40</a></sup> and were trimmed by trimAl (v.1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Capella-GutiÃ©rrez, S., Silla-MartÃ­nez, J. M. &amp; GabaldÃ³n, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972â1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2785">66</a></sup> to remove low-quality regions based on a heuristic approach (-automated1).</p><p>We then used RAxML (v.8.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2792">67</a></sup> with the GTR+G model to estimate a maximum-likelihood tree, starting with 200 rapid bootstraps followed by maximum-likelihood optimizations on every fifth bootstrap tree. Gene trees were rooted based on genes from <i>G. biloba</i> if these formed a monophyletic group in the tree; otherwise, mid-point rooting was applied. The timing of the duplication event for each anchor pair relative to the lineage divergence events was then inferred. In brief, internodes from a gene tree were first mapped to the species phylogeny according to the common ancestor of the genes in the gene tree. Each internode was then classified as a duplication node, a speciation node, or a node that has no paralogues and is inconsistent with divergence in the species phylogeny. The parental node(s) of a duplication node supported by an anchor pair were traced towards the root until reaching a speciation node in the gene tree. The duplication event that resulted in the anchor pair was hence circumscribed between the duplication node as the lower bound and the speciation node as the upper bound on the species tree. If the two nodes were directly connected by a single branch on the species tree, the duplication was thus considered to have occurred on the branch. To reduce biased estimations, we used the bootstrap value on the branch leading to a duplication node as support for a duplication event. In total, 497 anchor pairs in 473 gene families coalesced as duplication events on the species phylogeny, and duplication events from 254 anchor pairs in 246 gene families (or from 380 anchor pairs in 364 gene families) had bootstrap values greater than or equal to 80% (or 50%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Floral scent measurement, gene identification, and functional characterization</h3><p>We collected floral volatiles of <i>N. colorata</i> using a dynamic headspace sampling system and analysed them using gas chromatographyâmass spectrometry (GCâMS) as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li, G. et al. Nonseed plant Selaginella moellendorffi has both seed plant and microbial types of terpene synthases. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 14711â14715 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2811">68</a></sup>. After 2Â h of collection from the headspace of detached open flowers of <i>N. colorata</i> in a glass chamber (10 cm diameter, 30 cm height), volatiles were eluted from the SuperQ volatile collection trap using 100 Âµl of methylene chloride containing nonyl acetate as an internal standard. We then analysed samples using an Agilent Intuvo 9000 GC system coupled with an Agilent 7000D Triple Quadrupole mass detector. Separation was performed on an Agilent HP 5 MS capillary column (30 mÂ ÃÂ 0.25 mm) with helium as carrier gas (flow rate of 1 ml min<sup>â1</sup>). We applied splitless injections of 1 Âµl samples, injection temperature of 250âÂ°C, an initial oven temperature of 40âÂ°C (3-min hold) and a temperature gradient of 5âÂ°C per min increase from 40âÂ°C to 250âÂ°C. Products were identified using the National Institute of Standards and Technology mass spectral database (<a href="https://chemdata.nist.gov">https://chemdata.nist.gov</a>).</p><p>A full-length cDNA of NC11G0120830 was amplified from the open flowers of <i>N. colorata</i> using reverse transcription PCR (RTâPCR), and cloned into pET-32a (MilliporeSigma). After confirmation by sequencing, NC11G0120830 was expressed in <i>E. coli</i> strain BL21 (DE3) (Stratagene) and the recombinant protein produced was purified using a modified nickel-nitrilotriacetic acid agarose (Invitrogen) protocol as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Zhao, N., Guan, J., Lin, H. &amp; Chen, F. Molecular cloning and biochemical characterization of indole-3-acetic acid methyltransferase from poplar. Phytochemistry 68, 1537â1544 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2836">69</a></sup>. For methyltransferase enzyme assays, we used both radiochemical and non-radiochemical reaction systems. The radiochemical reaction system (50 Âµl) was composed of 50 mM Tris-HCl, pH 7.8, 1 mM substrate, 1 Âµl <sup>14</sup>C-<i>S</i>-adenosyl-<span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-methionine, and 1 Âµl of purified NC11G0120830. After 30 min of incubation at room temperature, 150 Âµl of ethyl acetate was added to extract the <sup>14</sup>C-labelled reaction products. The extracts were counted using a scintillation counter (Beckman Coulter) to measure the activity of NC11G0120830. To determine the chemical identity of the reaction product, we performed non-radiochemical assays in which nonradioactive <i>S</i>-adenosyl-<span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-methionine was used as the methyl donor. The reaction product was collected by headspace solid-phase microextraction and analysed by GCâMS as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Zhao, N. et al. Molecular and biochemical characterization of the jasmonic acid methyltransferase gene from black cottonwood (Populus trichocarpa). Phytochemistry 94, 74â81 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d17391e2857">70</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this paper.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>PacBio whole-genome sequencing data, Illumina data and genome assembly sequences have been deposited to the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) as Bioproject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA565347">PRJNA565347</a>, and were also deposited in the BIG Data Center (<a href="http://bigd.big.ac.cn">http://bigd.big.ac.cn</a>) under project number PRJCA001283. The genome assembly sequences and gene annotations have been deposited in the Genome Warehouse in BIG Data Center under accession number GWHAAYW00000000. The genome assembly sequences, gene annotations, and the LCN genes used in this study, have been also deposited in the Waterlily Pond (<a href="http://waterlily.eplant.org">http://waterlily.eplant.org</a>). All other data are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.</p>

Nymphaeales.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Intergenomic synteny between <i>N. colorata</i> (14 chromosomes), Five adjacent anchor pairs were plotted as one syntenic line. Coloured lines represent one example of syntenic genes found in other species that correspond to one copy in <i>Amborella</i>, two in <i>N. colorata</i>, two in <i>N. nucifera</i>, and three in <i>V. vinifera</i>. <b>b</b>, <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution for the whole paranome of <i>N. colorata</i>. The light grey rectangle in the background indicates the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> boundaries used to extract duplicate pairs for absolute phylogenomic dating of the WGD event, and also highlights the range in which WGD peaks can be identified in other species of Nymphaeaceae (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#MOESM1">5.2</a>). <b>c</b>, Kernel-density estimates of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions for one-to-one orthologues between the outgroup species <i>I. henryi</i> and each of <i>N. lutea</i> and <i>N. advena</i> (red), <i>N. colorata</i>, <i>N. mexicana</i> and <i>Nymphaea</i> âWoods blue goddessâ (blue) and <i>C. caroliniana</i> (yellow). As each peak represents the same divergence event in the angiosperm phylogeny, the differences observed among the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> valuesÂ of the peaks indicate substantial substitution rate variation among these Nymphaealean lineages (see also Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Fig2">2b</a>). <b>d</b>, Absolute age distribution obtained from phylogenomic dating of <i>N. colorata</i> WGD paralogues based on orthogroups with orthologues from <i>Amborella</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>. The solid black line represents the kernel density estimate of paralogue date estimates, and the vertical dashed black line represents its peak at 107Â Ma. The grey lines represent density estimates from 2,500 bootstrap replicates and the vertical black dotted lines represent the corresponding 90% confidence interval for the WGD age estimate, 117â98Â Ma (seeÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5#Sec2">Methods</a>). The blue histogram shows the raw distribution of divergence date estimates for paralogues.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-019-1852-5/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-019-1852-5/MediaObjects/41586_2019_1852_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 3 The phylogenetic tree of MADS-box genes of <i>N. colorata</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, The MADS-box genes are divided into type I and type II, and the latter was subdivided into MIKC<sup>c</sup> and MIKC*.
